<div id="review_391485317" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/391485317" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/391485317?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">the doldrums of cultural collapse</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nate D by: </span>
      murderous French aristocrats
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=france">france</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer391485317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11439250338660073641">Accepting dubious recommendations from the narrator of <i>The Marbled Swarm</i>, who perused a few pages of this in order to be able to appear to to have read it in conversation. <br/><br/>What is it? Essentially, sociological investigation and provocation through the lens of the international tourism economy:<br/><br/><blockquote>I liked holiday brochures, their abstraction, their way of condensing the places of the world into a limited sequence of possible pleasures and fares. I was particularly fond of the star rating system, whic</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText11439250338660073641" style="display:none">Accepting dubious recommendations from the narrator of <i>The Marbled Swarm</i>, who perused a few pages of this in order to be able to appear to to have read it in conversation. <br /><br />What is it? Essentially, sociological investigation and provocation through the lens of the international tourism economy:<br /><br /><blockquote>I liked holiday brochures, their abstraction, their way of condensing the places of the world into a limited sequence of possible pleasures and fares. I was particularly fond of the star rating system, which indicated the intensity of the pleasure one was entitled to hope for. I wasn't happy, but I valued happiness and continued to aspire to it. According to the Marshall model, the buyer is a rational individual seeking to maximize his satisfaction while taking price into consideration; Veblen's model, on the other hand, analyzes the effect of peer pressure on the buying process (depending on whether the buyer wishes to be identified with a defined group opr to set himself apart from it). Copeland demonstrates that the buying process varies, depending on the category of product/service (impulse purchase, considered purchase, specialized purchase); but the Buadrillard and Becker model posits that a purchase necessarily implies a series of signals. Overall, I felt myself closer to the Marshall model.</blockquote><br /><br />This is from near the start, where the prose seems most exceptional and refined, even in translation, where the deadened interior monologues of a middle-aged bureaucrat, a fundamentally repulsive non-person, are constantly intercut as above with statistical information and sociological data. He sounds like he could be a sociopath. It's harsh, hilarious, actually often piercing. It works. Almost every page we're blindsided by something like: "On an intellectual level, I was suddenly capable of acknowledging the attractions of the Muslim vagina."<br /><br />The prose style evens out after that at a somewhat more modest level, but the book is nothing if not carefully composed and directed throughout. Houellebecq can write, and he doesn't shy away from anything here; much of the books provocation is actually of thought. <br /><br />That said, the seemingly fundamental value in the book is simply that of pleasure, presented (cynically? naively?) as the one remaining avenue of genuine human contact in an increasingly desensitized and alienated western world, a world which continues to export its failing ideals all over the globe. And so, there's a lot of sex. Strangely, Houellebecq's cold, almost brusque cataloguing of sex acts actually worked pretty effectively here. He didn't grasp at awkward flights of lyricism, doesn't fall into the numbing boredom of overzealous lengthy description. He keeps things concise and direct and somehow convinced me, almost sentimentally, of some kind of real connection taking place. This is a widely debated point, if we go by other reviews, but I found it all more empathetic than, say, the oddly artificial sex culminating <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3610175.The_Girl_Beneath_the_Lion__Jupiter_Bks__" title="The Girl Beneath the Lion (Jupiter Bks.) by André Pieyre de Mandiargues" rel="nofollow noopener">The Girl Beneath the Lion</a> (freshest in my mind since I read it last week). Valerie, in any event, seems a far more effectual and sympathetic human than our narrator, though their paths coincide. What exactly ties her to him isn't entirely clear, it is true, but was oddly unworried by this while actually reading.<br /><br />So anyway, sexual pleasure is presented as the ideal here, almost, certainly to the characters who draft marketing taglines like "Pleasure is a right." I mean, shouldn't it be? Maybe? Does Houellebecq agree? Hard to say exactly, there're some terribly flawed social models here, unflinchingly presented, and though the social reformers are presented as unsympathetically as nearly everyone else, they also are "vindicated" in some way by actions that undercut everything else that has been built up. Throughout, Houellebecq's modeling of pleasure is precisely matched against apocalyptic social forces that the protagonists are inclined to utterly ignore as much as obliviously provoke. There's ambiguity as to what it all adds up to by inevitable finish, which deep analysis by others suggest is symptomatic of "idealogical double-crossing" and careful obfuscation of intent, but though I find it hard to disagree with Marie Redonnet here, I can't entirely dismiss Houellebecq as an asshole either. Or, if he's an asshole, he's one with talents, and possible uses.<br /><br />...<br /><br />Strangely, only a day later, I'm having trouble recalling why this wasn't a more annoying or problematic book to me while I was actually reading it, but somehow it wasn't.<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="11439250338660073641" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_166278134" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166278134" />
    <a title="Cbj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5283293-cbj"><img alt="Cbj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579249711p2/5283293.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/166278134?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cbj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cbj" href="/user/show/5283293-cbj">Cbj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5283293-cbj?shelf=alienation">alienation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5283293-cbj?shelf=tired-of-modern-life">tired-of-modern-life</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5283293-cbj?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166278134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16591050772223782671"><b>Platform</b> follows the life of Michel, a middle aged French government employee, told in first person. The novel starts with the following line : "<i>Father died last year. I don't subscribe to the notion that we only become truly adult when our parents die; we never become truly adult.</i>" It is a nod to <b>The Outsider</b>.<br/><br/>Michel is a detached, frustrated and sex obsessed man whose father is murdered by a conservative Muslim. Michel's father was having an affair with the man's sister. Michel goes on a sex to</span>
  <span id="freeText16591050772223782671" style="display:none"><b>Platform</b> follows the life of Michel, a middle aged French government employee, told in first person. The novel starts with the following line : "<i>Father died last year. I don't subscribe to the notion that we only become truly adult when our parents die; we never become truly adult.</i>" It is a nod to <b>The Outsider</b>.<br /><br />Michel is a detached, frustrated and sex obsessed man whose father is murdered by a conservative Muslim. Michel's father was having an affair with the man's sister. Michel goes on a sex tour to Thailand using the money his father left him. Houellebecq says that Europeans don't have sex anymore. Feminism, nihilism and individuality have left Europeans lonely and detached while men and women hate each other. The family system has broken up thanks to the hedonism of the 60s. Free market capitalism has left people doing mind numbing jobs - they spend a good part of their lives working in ridiculous jobs unable to form meaningful relationships with the people around them. Life has no meaning anymore. In such a situation the individual is in a continuous state of sexual neuroses because the only path to some comfort and tenderness from the horror of modern life seems to be through sex. But sex is not freely available in Europe. A substantial portion of young men and women in Europe do not have sexual partners. So they travel to places like Thailand for sex where sex has become a buy able commodity.<br /><br />And then there is the looming threat of Islam. Houellebecq takes an extremely negative view of Islam and its impact on societies.<br /><br />There are long monologues about sex - <i>offering your body as an object of pleasure, giving pleasure unselfishly; that's what Westerners don't know how to do anymore</i>, racism - <i>a more aggressive sense of competition between males of different races; but the corollary is an increased sense of competition for the females of the other race</i>, capitalism and modern life.<br /><br />Platform is a pretty provocative novel with a lot of ideas about why the world is the way it is today. My only problem with it was a lack of subtlety or lack of craftsmanship. Sometimes characters just take off on long monologues. I posted this review on another book forum and the European members told me that Houellebecq exaggerated the level of hostility between men and women in Europe.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16591050772223782671" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3638878" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3638878" />
    <a title="Carolyn Heinze" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/114533-carolyn-heinze"><img alt="Carolyn Heinze" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1366541599p2/114533.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3638878?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carolyn Heinze" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carolyn Heinze" href="/user/show/114533-carolyn-heinze">Carolyn Heinze</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782290321232" href="/book/show/58378.Plateforme">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/114533-carolyn-heinze?shelf=lifestooshort">lifestooshort</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3638878" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17358983851999889336">Picked this up out of curiousity, and I can see why he is such a hit in France. There is a category of people here that embraces forced cynicism and intellectual masturbation. Frankly, I couldn't find anything smart about it, but perhaps I'm limited. Funny, I got to page 107 before deciding that time is too precious to waste on something I don't like - the same number of pages I read of Céline's equally irritating Voyage au bout de la nuit - a staple of French literature (and a book one shouldn'</span>
  <span id="freeText17358983851999889336" style="display:none">Picked this up out of curiousity, and I can see why he is such a hit in France. There is a category of people here that embraces forced cynicism and intellectual masturbation. Frankly, I couldn't find anything smart about it, but perhaps I'm limited. Funny, I got to page 107 before deciding that time is too precious to waste on something I don't like - the same number of pages I read of Céline's equally irritating Voyage au bout de la nuit - a staple of French literature (and a book one shouldn't admit to loathing at most soirées). I suspect M. Houellebecq is a huge Céline fan, and he probably likes Sade and Henry Miller as well. It's been a long time since I've read the latter two, however I do remember that within their sometimes hardcore delivery, there was some sensuality and wit, or, I don't know, skill. I think he was trying to be satirical, but it just doesn't come through. Instead, he comes off as a sullen teenager who didn't succeed in getting his curfew extended. M. Houellebecq, in my opinion, doesn't have the chops. The star's for the marketing coup. Good for him - we'll probably continue to see his mug on the extensive circuit of French talk shows that pander to those in his element.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17358983851999889336" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1168490632" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1168490632" />
    <a title="Paul A." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24838580-paul-a"><img alt="Paul A." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472996552p2/24838580.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1168490632?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul A." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul A." href="/user/show/24838580-paul-a">Paul A.</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer4136547621328364512">Sprachlos... <br/><br/>After reading this novel, I confirm that Houellebecq is the kind of writers that is hard to find nowadays. He writes about how he see the world, he doesn't write what other want to read. Nowadays, most of the writers are just business man, trying to sell their books without compromise them selfs exposing a philosophy or and idea.   <span id="freeText4136547621328364512" style="display:none">Sprachlos... <br /><br />After reading this novel, I confirm that Houellebecq is the kind of writers that is hard to find nowadays. He writes about how he see the world, he doesn't write what other want to read. Nowadays, most of the writers are just business man, trying to sell their books without compromise them selfs exposing a philosophy or and idea. Houellebecq just write what he thinks, analyzing the present, and projecting to the future. <br /><br />In Platform we can read strong compliments against occident, the European society, the capitalism, religions (Muslims and Christians). And It's funny to know that Houellebecq is accused of being a misogynist, but what he writes is that the world or it would be better to say, the Capitalism, is misogynist, the religion is misogynist. I can't really deal with the "Good-thinker" people, that don't try to understand what they read, they are just there to condemn all what can shock the society. But we need writer as Houellebecq, they are compromise with the world. Beside his misanthropy, he tries to expose why he "hates" the world.<br /><br />This is not a book just about sex, as The Elementary Particles, this is an analysis of our present world.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1879175167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1879175167" />
    <a title="Bruno Inácio" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25863961-bruno-in-cio"><img alt="Bruno Inácio" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487450066p2/25863961.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1879175167?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bruno Inácio" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bruno Inácio" href="/user/show/25863961-bruno-in-cio">Bruno Inácio</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25863961-bruno-in-cio?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25863961-bruno-in-cio?shelf=culture">culture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25863961-bruno-in-cio?shelf=eroticism">eroticism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25863961-bruno-in-cio?shelf=transgressive">transgressive</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25863961-bruno-in-cio?shelf=sexuality">sexuality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25863961-bruno-in-cio?shelf=decadent">decadent</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1879175167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11842804570814605059">"Platform" is, very succinctly, a love story, which I wasn't expecting to conclude when I first read about the misogyny, the islamophobia and the overall polemics surrounding this book. On the other hand, it felt very familiar to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/58314.The_Elementary_Particles' title='The Elementary Particles by Michel Houellebecq' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Elementary Particles</a> when it came to the type of relationship between the narrator and his partner. <br/><br/>The writing is very "hoarse", the sex scenes are essentially pornographic with things being called by their names and lasting for a paragraph or two. Still, there are</span>
  <span id="freeText11842804570814605059" style="display:none">"Platform" is, very succinctly, a love story, which I wasn't expecting to conclude when I first read about the misogyny, the islamophobia and the overall polemics surrounding this book. On the other hand, it felt very familiar to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/58314.The_Elementary_Particles" title="The Elementary Particles by Michel Houellebecq" rel="nofollow noopener">The Elementary Particles</a> when it came to the type of relationship between the narrator and his partner. <br /><br />The writing is very "hoarse", the sex scenes are essentially pornographic with things being called by their names and lasting for a paragraph or two. Still, there are many instances of a more, let's call it, poetic usage of language, moments of lucidity that bring some light to the overall bleakness of his outlook on life. <br /><br />Houellebecq said in an interview to the BBC that you need more motivation than misogyny to write a novel, almost waving the matter away, which is a pretty good point. I would add that if you're a woman-hater, this book ain't for you. The racist, misogynist Robert, in the first part of the book, is viewed by the narrator with a vague curiosity, quickly sated, while his partner, Valérie, is wary of the 50-something polemic right-winger. At last, the narrator concludes that what he sees in Robert is his own future as a failure whose only options left are the squandering of money in the Third World, buying the women of other races. A defeated general in a largely imagined race war where you fight for the bodies of females. <br /><br />What saves Michel, the narrator, from this is Valérie. One could say that she is too perfect, an idealized woman and, therefore, a product of misogyny. Gradually, this idea seemed inaccurate, unfitting to this woman. She is young and quite successful but that has nothing to do with how she pleases Michel. Rarely he points out any specific features on her body that are a result of her young years. On the contrary, her youth, coupled with her career, reflect her predatory nature. Sexually, she is very sovereign over Michel. The almost automatic way in which she is willing to pleasure him is not dumb submission but sovereignty. He is like a toy with which she plays in an empty street, a train not unoccupied or in the hotel room, with the cleaning lady. The way he almost passes out and screams unconsciously while orgasming betray a lack of control. <br /><br />At the same time, Valéries doesn't seem particularly interested in BDSM, clearly put off by the pain, the latex and the rules. Her sexuality is one of giving, squandering, without much thought. There is a solar quality that affects the whole structure of the book.<br /><br />With her the idea behind the utopian sex tourism project begins to shape itself in Michel's mind. Of course, it is a thing ridden with ethical problems but to think that Houellebecq is, somehow, trying to justify any of it seems poorly thought. Again, you need more motivation than that to write a novel. If Thai massage parlours where the answer, Michel would not need Valérie to save him. Put in another way, he would not give the prostitutes in Thailand a second chance after losing Valérie. The book is very explicit about this: after the possibility of love was lost, everything else was as well. The very thing thought to bring Europeans out of their torpor was revealed to be absolutely useless to Michel in the end. Only his biological death waited him.<br /><br />"Love sanctifies" says Michel, somewhere along the second part. Once that is over, how much is not also over.<br /><br />That's why I believe this is, above everything else, a love story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11842804570814605059" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_505292069" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/505292069" />
    <a title="Michael Meeuwis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8841929-michael-meeuwis"><img alt="Michael Meeuwis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352646560p2/8841929.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/505292069?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Meeuwis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Meeuwis" href="/user/show/8841929-michael-meeuwis">Michael Meeuwis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer505292069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2882497235047843104">One and a half stars, and I'll explain why at the end. Reads like a cross between "Penthouse Letters," Zizek's C-level material, and--for one vertiginous stretch in the middle--Thomas Friedman at his hottest and flattest. Pauvre Michel is having sex-life problems, until he has sex with a Thai prostitute--apparently third-world women can still make the sex properly. (Their economic privation has saved them from western-style alienation.) Then he meets Valerie, a first-world woman who--néanmoins--</span>
  <span id="freeText2882497235047843104" style="display:none">One and a half stars, and I'll explain why at the end. Reads like a cross between "Penthouse Letters," Zizek's C-level material, and--for one vertiginous stretch in the middle--Thomas Friedman at his hottest and flattest. Pauvre Michel is having sex-life problems, until he has sex with a Thai prostitute--apparently third-world women can still make the sex properly. (Their economic privation has saved them from western-style alienation.) Then he meets Valerie, a first-world woman who--néanmoins--is selfless enough to pleasure him in the third-world manner. (I can furnish the curious with the page where he says this.) Val works for a tour company, and Michel makes a world-changing suggestion: run a world-wide series of hotels where first-world men and women can have sex with third-world men and women and their "purer" (his word, not mine) sexuality. Everyone benefits from the arrangement: the sex toured get money for practicing their weightless sexuality; the sex tourists get laid. In terms of its attitude towards the interior life of Asian persons, this makes "The Quiet American" look like "The Joy Luck Club." I mean, Christ on a battery hen: at least Gaugin put spooky symbols in his backgrounds. <br /><br />Oh, and the whole arrangement is ruined by Muslims. And the book is dotted with Muslim spokespeople who handily explain how stupid Islam is.<br /><br />The foreign, brown (his word) woman are always wet (his word) and have pretty much prehensile genitalia--things that western women have lost, apparently, in their drive not to be entirely economically dependent on sexual transactions. A maid watches Mike and Val going at it, and wanders into the room herself, blouse a-flutter and nipples (his word) a-twitter. The main character reads Comte. There are sex clubs. This is all much less interesting than it sounds.<br /><br />I started this out of curiosity, and finished it out of spite. I would give it book one star, but that would make it sound more shocking or just reaction-getting than it is. I'm vaguely terrified that people take take this seriously--Wikipedia tells me there was a theater adaptation--or think that this Michel dude (same name, writer and main character, wink wink) has insights into postwhatever society. So one and a half stars for the breast and a half on the cover. It's a very nice breast and a half. <br /><br />Of course, this is French, so for all I know there's some subtle tone in the original which indicates that this is all some sort of sophisticated joke on the sort of person who would write a review like this. I can live with that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2882497235047843104" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1315800752" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1315800752" />
    <a title="Derek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27332705-derek"><img alt="Derek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525338173p2/27332705.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1315800752?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Derek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Derek" href="/user/show/27332705-derek">Derek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27332705-derek?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27332705-derek?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27332705-derek?shelf=highly-recommended">highly-recommended</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1315800752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17040135773318356990">This is a surprise literary gem... If you excuse the masochism and the graphic sexuality (which shouldn't be a surprise, considering the bikini-clad hottie on the cover) Essentially, it's a story about love and loss and moving on (or not) It's about sexuality, about western ideals versus eastern and third world. The world we're living in, at least for the narrator, is a bleak one when it comes to gender roles. A lot of questions are asked in regards to the attractiveness of sexual tourism, witho</span>
  <span id="freeText17040135773318356990" style="display:none">This is a surprise literary gem... If you excuse the masochism and the graphic sexuality (which shouldn't be a surprise, considering the bikini-clad hottie on the cover) Essentially, it's a story about love and loss and moving on (or not) It's about sexuality, about western ideals versus eastern and third world. The world we're living in, at least for the narrator, is a bleak one when it comes to gender roles. A lot of questions are asked in regards to the attractiveness of sexual tourism, without it being some skewed moral debate. And yes, I must confess, Michel makes some strong, pertinent points, and yes, I've marked a couple of destinations I'm curious to vacation in, if I can afford it. But all that aside. What Valerie and Michel have, well, who wouldn't want that. It's highly sexual and risqué, but it suits them. And all the characters in the novel are well-rounded, they're not cardboards (unlike the characters in Henry Miller's Opus Pistorum) they've depth and dimension and great potential for empathy. It makes what happened to them in the end rather sad and heartbreaking. But that's the kind of ending I love. This wasn't meant to be a happily ever after fairy tale. <br />Oh, and his 'burn' of Islam, whoa! Was that coherent thinking or what!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17040135773318356990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Thoroughly unpleasant, not much of a payoff... and yet, I like it and I’ll keep reading him. Dunno what’s wrong with me...</span>

<div id="review_3225843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3225843" />
    <a title="elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54800-elizabeth"><img alt="elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1176188517p2/54800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3225843?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="elizabeth" href="/user/show/54800-elizabeth">elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782080682376" href="/book/show/1518409.Plateforme">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">misogynist pricks </span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3225843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7493938059395924324">Houellebecq has his moments of funny, particularly in describing the merdique travails of modern air travel. And it's not his statement in this book that Islam is a violent religion that particularly earned my vitriol. Nor is it his "provocative" thesis that the only way to save the French tourism industry is through sex tourism, though both of those seem to have done it for a number of readers. Clever man that he is, Houellebecq sets up his provocations so that any critics just sound like shril</span>
  <span id="freeText7493938059395924324" style="display:none">Houellebecq has his moments of funny, particularly in describing the merdique travails of modern air travel. And it's not his statement in this book that Islam is a violent religion that particularly earned my vitriol. Nor is it his "provocative" thesis that the only way to save the French tourism industry is through sex tourism, though both of those seem to have done it for a number of readers. Clever man that he is, Houellebecq sets up his provocations so that any critics just sound like shrill humorless feminists who don't get the joke. Yet his provocations come off as, at best, locker room fantasies. And the casual way in which every sexually appealing female is raped or blown up, suggests to this humorless feminist that Monsieur H has not been laid in a very long time. Which should be a relief to women everywhere.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7493938059395924324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2427857567" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2427857567" />
    <a title="Joseph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38094702-joseph"><img alt="Joseph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419021894p2/38094702.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2427857567?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joseph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joseph" href="/user/show/38094702-joseph">Joseph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2427857567" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14900939669702792608">His first book, “Whatever”, was readable and had some insightful, often beautiful passages, I considered the racist words to be the characters' and not the authors', and they worked with the tone of despair and isolation.<br/><br/>I haven't read his second or any other of his books. “Platform” is his third book and it's just unimaginative pornography and racism, with an occasional interesting sociological or anthropological observation, that is his strong point. He should have been an academic but must ha</span>
  <span id="freeText14900939669702792608" style="display:none">His first book, “Whatever”, was readable and had some insightful, often beautiful passages, I considered the racist words to be the characters' and not the authors', and they worked with the tone of despair and isolation.<br /><br />I haven't read his second or any other of his books. “Platform” is his third book and it's just unimaginative pornography and racism, with an occasional interesting sociological or anthropological observation, that is his strong point. He should have been an academic but must have had conceits about his artistic ability. In this book he drops his mask and reveals that the racism from the first book is part of his character and beliefs - the protagonist is even called Michel. His is a crude and hypocritical hatred, where he also exposes his ignorance about the targets of his hatred: mainly Islam and violent French teenage gangs of blacks and Muslims, but also obviously women and probably all of humanity except for whores. <br /><br />In one particularly appalling and idiotic passage, a westernized Egyptian chemist is used as a cheap diversion to mouth the author's own stupidity. The chemist laments the loss of ancient Egyptian polytheism with all of its cultural and scientific achievements (as well as its beautiful scantily clad women like Cleopatra!); he tells us, wrongly of course, that Islam achieved nothing and that the women are now fat lumps covered in a sack of “rags”(burkhas, I guess). Houellebecq clearly doesn't know or care that fat women are considered more attractive in many mid-eastern cultures. The chemist goes on to say that Islam came from "barbaric" desert tribes and that nothing good ever came out of the desert. I think it's safe to say that ancient Egypt, as well as Carthage, were nothing more than narrow fertile strips surrounded by desert. Then, trying to give his ignorance the semblance of an intellectual thought, the chemist (Houellebecq) claims that the root of the problem is monotheism, and to prove western superiority, he clumsily lets Catholicism off the hook by saying it's really polytheism, because of the Trinity, Mary, the saints and the angels and that the Church fathers designed it that way intentionally; that they reverted it to polytheism/paganism. There is however, no clever explanation of Protestantism. I guess they also achieved nothing.<br /><br />This is the lowest level of ignorance, especially coming from a man who makes claims to be an artist and an intellectual. Islam is not one generalized thing. There are many sects and variations and many people who could give a fuck about it in the so-called Islamic world. The object of his hatred is probably the fundamentalist Wahabi Sunni sect funded by the Saudis, those responsible for ISIS, the Taliban, Boko Haram and Al Qaida. Sunni Islam and Islam in general, does revere saints and angels - this is common knowledge. The Wahabi-Salafists, along with other countless atrocities, also destroy the tombs and monuments of these saints whenever they get enough funding to go on a rampage as a political death squad, fulfilling the sick ambitions of some political/financial elite. Houllebecq, a person who tries to give the impression he's read practically everything you never have, doesn't know that Islam reveres angels, including the Christian archangels? He either doesn't know, or else is omitting the fact to qualify this hateful passage, reducing him to nothing more than a liar, cynically playing to many of his readers' ignorance and lack of critical thinking ability. His editors and publisher must also share in this disgrace.<br /><br />MH's racism takes on many forms: from the banal garden-variety shock value stuff of the yellow press, where he lists a plague of attacks and rapes and talks about "barbarian hoards" in the suburbs, to more subtle, more disgusting stuff, where he includes black people in his sordid porno scenes, and where Huoellebecq absurdly and incredibly portrays himself as the stud. One scene has him and his dreamy French lover Valerie, inviting a black Cuban maid to have sex with them. They throw her some cash afterwards but he gives the hint that she would have done it for free! In another, they go to a swinger's club and hook-up with a "nice" black couple. There is an especially revolting passage where he and the black guy ( who is a jazz drummer!) discuss musical time signatures while the other's girlfriends suck them off – A jazz drummer! Houellebecq truly wants to shit on everything. Earlier on in the book - in the Thailand vacation part - he ridicules people who are concerned about child prostitution and sex tourism, claiming that they're hysterical phonies and that sex tourism is a result of the frigidity of western women and the pressures of its society.<br /><br />I have rarely encountered a person whose obnoxiousness comes through more clearly in something claiming to be, and marketed as a work of art. Celine got banished because of his racism and this weasel wins literary prizes for his, though he's not worthy to even be mentioned along with Celine - he's the Damien Hirst of writing. We are deep in the shit when most critics can't see the obvious weaknesses and failings in writing like this. Instead, they think it's "cutting edge" and "controversial". We suffer a plague of idiots in the arts, as well as in politics; but at least they've always been at home there. <br /><br />The writing gets human for a brief period, after the terrorist attack, when he grieves Valerie and describes his depression, but with a character as unlikable as Michel, it doesn't connect with the rest of the story, so it feels hollow. Perhaps it's the point where the author's true emptiness and despair come through with some sincerity. <br /><br />MH's main premise is that sexual pleasure is all there is in life to look forward to. Not children, not work, not art, not even traveling unless it's to find whores . Only ejaculations matter - You can' t even call them orgasms. He relies on generalizing qualities such as pleasure, that are very specific and nuanced. Many people don't consider sex with hookers pleasurable; many do it with a sense of disgust, out of desperation and loneliness; it often makes the situation worse. Only Houllebecq seems to think it is perfectly natural. He forces this presumption on the reader. He portrays sex tourism as a utopia and dismisses the general view that it's sordid and exploitative. In his fundamentally racist worldview, he claims the women like it and were in a sense, born to sexually serve whites. He spells this view out quite clearly several times. Usually, the men that travel to these places for sex are considered the worst creeps and cynical losers. MH tries to make them romantic figures. The only time this shit bird shows empathy is for these Johns and pedos. He claims that every creep in the world deserves or has a right to feel sexual pleasure and this is his essential philosophical and ethical failure - SOME PEOPLE DESERVE TO SUFFER! You stand corrected Houellebecq - call me a Catholic pagan if you must. <br /><br />MH's philosophy of pleasure through prostitution, would necessitate maintaining and increasing the economic subjugation of much of the world, to Western and other rich nations. He advocates and predicts the destruction of Islam, and has a character absurdly claim that it's doomed because young Muslims desire sex and designer stuff. I wonder if Houellebecq secretly advocates faster, more efficient, more effective ways to destroy Islam?</span>
  <a data-text-id="14900939669702792608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

	<tr>
		<td>
		    <b>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('x+SMeOBm/yJKpWWP8bk5gLJF7cUb8/9dB6cIQiv6paUMBDcwiTZ9ywY7qoa/7+sYoqoWqA1QGne+moDzIgszyg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Uzj+bZRivclNbGFrNPQ+L+55zL2lCDv18dBLQHEtEX2Y2EUl/TI/IAHyrmJ6ouy3/pY30LOr3t9I7cPxeNyHEg==')})
  }

A violent showdown in the Sonora desert turns search to flight; twenty years later Belano and Lima are still on the run.\n\nThe explosive first long work by “the most exciting writer to come from south of the Rio Grande in a long time” (Ilan Stavans, Los Angeles Times), The Savage Detectives follows Belano and Lima through the eyes of the people whose paths they cross in Central America, Europe, Israel, and West Africa. This chorus includes the muses of visceral realism, the beautiful Font sisters; their father, an architect interned in a Mexico City asylum; a sensitive young follower of Octavio Paz; a foul-mouthed American graduate student; a French girl with a taste for the Marquis de Sade; the great-granddaughter of Leon Trotsky; a Chilean stowaway with a mystical gift for numbers; the anorexic heiress to a Mexican underwear empire; an Argentinian photojournalist in Angola; and assorted hangers-on, detractors, critics, lovers, employers, vagabonds, real-life literary figures, and random acquaintances.\n\nA polymathic descendant of Borges and Pynchon, Roberto Bolaño traces the hidden connection between literature and violence in a world where national boundaries are fluid and death lurks in the shadow of the avant-garde. Veering between nervous excitability and debilitating ennui, he gluts his aesthetic appetites with classical literature and art, exotic jewels (with which he fatally encrusts the shell of his tortoise), rich perfumes, and a kaleidoscope of sensual experiences.       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_12395'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12395.Journey_to_the_End_of_the_Night?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Journey to the End of the Night<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/7869.Louis_Ferdinand_C_line?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Louis-Ferdinand Céline<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.22 avg rating &mdash; 32,141 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1932\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer2846074726308513483\">Louis-Ferdinand Celine\'s revulsion and anger at what he considered the idiocy and hypocrisy of society explodes from nearly every page of this novel. Filled with slang and obscenities and written in raw, colloquial language, Journey to the End of the<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2846074726308513483\" style=\"display:none\">Louis-Ferdinand Celine\'s revulsion and anger at what he considered the idiocy and hypocrisy of society explodes from nearly every page of this novel. Filled with slang and obscenities and written in raw, colloquial language, Journey to the End of the Night is a literary symphony of violence, cruelty and obscene nihilism. This book shocked most critics when it was first published in France in 1932, but quickly became a success with the reading public in Europe, and later in America where it was first published by New Directions in 1952. The story of the improbable yet convincingly described travels of the petit-bourgeois (and largely autobiographical) antihero, Bardamu, from the trenches of World War I, to the African jungle, to New       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/32878.Michel_Houellebecq">About Michel Houellebecq</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9455635681775480308">Michel Houellebecq (born Michel Thomas), born 26 February 1958 (birth certificate) or 1956 on the French island of Réunion, is a controversial and award-winning French novelist. To admirers he is a writer in the tradition of literary provocation that reaches back to the Marquis de Sade and Baudelaire; to detractors he is a peddler, who writes vulgar sleazy literature to shock. His works though, pa</span>
  <span id="freeText9455635681775480308" style="display:none">Michel Houellebecq (born Michel Thomas), born 26 February 1958 (birth certificate) or 1956 on the French island of Réunion, is a controversial and award-winning French novelist. To admirers he is a writer in the tradition of literary provocation that reaches back to the Marquis de Sade and Baudelaire; to detractors he is a peddler, who writes vulgar sleazy literature to shock. His works though, particularly Atomised, have received high praise from the French literary intelligentsia, with generally positive international critical response, Having written poetry and a biography of the horror writer H. P. Lovecraft, he brought out his first novel <i>Extension du domaine de la lutte</i> in 1994. <i>Les particules élémentaires</i> followed in 1998 and <i>Plateforme</i>, in 2001. After a disastrous publicity tour for this book, which led to his being taken to court for inciting racial hatred, he went to Ireland to write. He currently resides in France, where he has been described as "France’s biggest literary export and, some say, greatest living writer". In 2010 he published <i>La Carte et le Territoire</i> (published the same year in English as <i>The Map and the Territory</i>) which won the prestigious Prix Goncourt; and, in 2015, <i>Submission</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9455635681775480308" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
